  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

<span id="freeTextContainer5064133455016700839">Betsy and I are fighting right now. You see, I just can't forgive her for lousing it up with Joe. Of course we all know it'll work out right in the end because, in the words of that annoying girl from <i>Sleepless in Seattle</i> they're "MFEO." But for now, I call you out Betsy Ray. You have permission to do the same for me when I inevitably make my own romantic blunders. That is, you could, if you were a real person. Who would probably be dead from old age by now. Regardless.<br/><br/>I'm also fighting with Joe</span>
  <span id="freeText5064133455016700839" style="display:none">Betsy and I are fighting right now. You see, I just can't forgive her for lousing it up with Joe. Of course we all know it'll work out right in the end because, in the words of that annoying girl from <i>Sleepless in Seattle</i> they're "MFEO." But for now, I call you out Betsy Ray. You have permission to do the same for me when I inevitably make my own romantic blunders. That is, you could, if you were a real person. Who would probably be dead from old age by now. Regardless.<br /><br />I'm also fighting with Joe because I can't forgive him for the mustache. A mustache? No, you will regret this, sir. If I've learned anything from romcoms, it's that facial hair incurred during a break-up is never good news.</span>

<div id="review_6008712" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6008712" />
    <a title="Melody" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/369112-melody"><img alt="Melody" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1291050708p2/369112.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6008712?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melody" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melody" href="/user/show/369112-melody">Melody</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780690135916" href="/book/show/6506578-betsy-and-the-great-world">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/369112-melody?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/369112-melody?shelf=comfort-read">comfort-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6008712" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1720476098264175131">09/2015 -stet<br/><br/>09/2012 Wendy asked me to review this from the point of view of someone without a passport, so...<br/>I love visiting pre-WWI Europe with Betsy, much the way I love visiting pre-WWII Greece with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/26957.Gerald_Durrell' title='Gerald Durrell' rel='nofollow noopener'>Gerald Durrell</a>. I think that I will never see post WWII anything save the US, and it does make me sad, though I do think I get as much out of reading as a lot of people get out of actually being there. <br/><br/>I love Betsy here although her dependence on men (any man! the passing man, the friendly nephew</span>
  <span id="freeText1720476098264175131" style="display:none">09/2015 -stet<br /><br />09/2012 Wendy asked me to review this from the point of view of someone without a passport, so...<br />I love visiting pre-WWI Europe with Betsy, much the way I love visiting pre-WWII Greece with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/26957.Gerald_Durrell" title="Gerald Durrell" rel="nofollow noopener">Gerald Durrell</a>. I think that I will never see post WWII anything save the US, and it does make me sad, though I do think I get as much out of reading as a lot of people get out of actually being there. <br /><br />I love Betsy here although her dependence on men (any man! the passing man, the friendly nephew of the house, the millionaire aboard) is grating. The chapter from London makes me weep. The last few paragraphs make me stand up and shout.<br /><br />Such a lovely, lovely book. I wish I could see the world that Betsy saw.<br /><br /><br />12/2009 When I was reading and re-reading this series every season as a kid, this is the book I flew through. I skimmed the tedious descriptions of pre-WWI Europe with a yawn, pined for Tacy and Tib and rolled my eyes at everything but Marco, Mrs. Main-Whittaker, and of course the glorious, perfect last chapter.<br /><br />This book began to change for me when I was in my mid-twenties, and it's become one of my favorites in the series. The rich and beautiful descriptions of a Europe forever vanished make me wistful, and so grateful that Maud chose to do so little foreshadowing. I still love Marco, though he wasn't right for Betsy. I'm more fascinated with Mrs. Main-Whittaker now that I know she was modeled on Rose Wilder Lane, and I simply adore this book start to finish. Betsy's Europe is idyllic in a lot of ways, even when she's homesick and lost. It annoys me that she's constantly being bailed out by gentlemen, too- but that's true to Betsy, and I have to honor that.<br /><br />But most of all... Best.Last.Chapter.Ever. Ever. No, I mean it. Ever.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1720476098264175131" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2913887773" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2664477711634252266">I missed Deep Valley so much, but I loved reading about Betsy's adventures abroad! I especially enjoyed her time in Italy. I loved Marco! Betsy was a strong woman to not fall in love with him - such a romantic italian. I mean, I LOVE Joe, don't get me wrong, but reading about Marco also gave me butterflies. She had a hard choice to make. She ultimately made the right choice, and now I can have Marco to myself.<br/><br/>It was interesting to read the book knowing that WWI was right around the corner. There</span>
  <span id="freeText2664477711634252266" style="display:none">I missed Deep Valley so much, but I loved reading about Betsy's adventures abroad! I especially enjoyed her time in Italy. I loved Marco! Betsy was a strong woman to not fall in love with him - such a romantic italian. I mean, I LOVE Joe, don't get me wrong, but reading about Marco also gave me butterflies. She had a hard choice to make. She ultimately made the right choice, and now I can have Marco to myself.<br /><br />It was interesting to read the book knowing that WWI was right around the corner. There were hints in the story that it was coming, but the benefit of historical fiction is knowing what's going to happen before it does. Her time in Europe (Germany and Italy especially) is mostly carefree and idyllic until she reaches London and the war breaks out. It lended some poignancy to the story knowing that those beautiful people and places she just spent so much time in are now caught up in the crushing hands of fate and will never be the same.<br /><br />But oh my goodness does Betsy have some pride that she needs to learn to swallow. She knew she'd hurt Joe but refused to write to him and apologize which was just SO INFURIATING. Actually, both Betsy and Joe have a streak of pride in them that is not particularly flattering and they'll have to work on in their future marriage. Not to get all preachy, but fessing up when you're wrong and apologizing is like relationship 101. But no. Betsy needed to find an EXCUSE in order to write to Joe, and even when she does finally write him, she doesn't apologize. Just. Betsy. Come on. But, their reconciliation is still heart melting and Joe's response to her in the newspaper was swoony. Once I finished the book I had to pick up the next one right away just so I could witness their reunion immediately. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2664477711634252266" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_8083663" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/8083663" />
    <a title="Carmen Maloy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/563625-carmen-maloy"><img alt="Carmen Maloy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1195667618p2/563625.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/8083663?book_show_action=true">Oct 22, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carmen Maloy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carmen Maloy" href="/user/show/563625-carmen-maloy">Carmen Maloy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Everyone</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer8083663" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17675651784843279983">I have to say that this is probably my least favorite of the series. I still love it, but there are things lacking such as all the fun surrounding the "crowd" and Betsy's beloved Deep Valley. That being said, it's still a great book and a must read. At twenty-one, Betsy is on the verge of a great adventure. She embarks on a tour of Europe - when Europe was a new horizon for a young writer to see. Betsy soon discovers that she is growing up (she is courted by a dashing Italian during her trip abr</span>
  <span id="freeText17675651784843279983" style="display:none">I have to say that this is probably my least favorite of the series. I still love it, but there are things lacking such as all the fun surrounding the "crowd" and Betsy's beloved Deep Valley. That being said, it's still a great book and a must read. At twenty-one, Betsy is on the verge of a great adventure. She embarks on a tour of Europe - when Europe was a new horizon for a young writer to see. Betsy soon discovers that she is growing up (she is courted by a dashing Italian during her trip abroad), but she can not forget her beloved Joe.<br /><br />The Betsy-Tacy books were highly autobiographical and Lovelace perfectly captures the innocence and magic of childhood. If you read this book and love it, please read the series. It will be books that you will never forget as long as you live. I also recommend the "Betsy-Tacy Companion" which is an amazing book that disects each book and compares it to it's real-life counterparts, including pictures of the "real" Betsy, Tacy, Tib and all the gang. <br /><br />I had the pleasure of visiting "Deep Valley" (aka Mankato, Minnesota) for a Betsy-Tacy convention back in 1996. It was incredible to step back in time and enter Betsy's world. We toured the city and I was actually able to step foot in "Tacy's" bedroom and sit on the famous bench at the top of the big hill. It was truly a life-altering experience. I have to thank my sister, Julie for introducting these books to me and changing my life. <br /><br />It's obvious how much these books mean to me. My first born child was named Tacy Kelly Maloy. Please read and enjoy. They are a treasure! <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17675651784843279983" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3081275479" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3081275479" />
    <a title="Abigail" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/87835420-abigail"><img alt="Abigail" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1539121384p2/87835420.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3081275479?book_show_action=true">Dec 11, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Abigail" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Abigail" href="/user/show/87835420-abigail">Abigail</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/15939538-betsy-and-the-great-world">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Betsy Tacy Fans</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/87835420-abigail?shelf=americans-abroad">americans-abroad</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/87835420-abigail?shelf=childrens-fiction">childrens-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/87835420-abigail?shelf=vintage-girls">vintage-girls</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/87835420-abigail?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/87835420-abigail?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/87835420-abigail?shelf=wwi">wwi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/87835420-abigail?shelf=minnesota">minnesota</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3081275479" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7282148867920864569">Opening three years after the events of <u>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7898.Betsy_and_Joe__Betsy_Tacy___8_' title='Betsy and Joe (Betsy-Tacy, #8) by Maud Hart Lovelace' rel='nofollow noopener'>Betsy and Joe</a>
</u>, the previous installment of Maud Hart Lovelace's <i>Betsy~Tacy</i> series, <u>Betsy and the Great World</u> sees its lovable heroine - now twenty-one years old, and about to be a junior in college - head off for a trip to Europe. Crossing the Atlantic on the Columbic ocean-liner, living in a student pension in Munich, exploring Venice with a handsome young man, and facing the onset of World War I with her friends in London, Betsy has many exciting adv</span>
  <span id="freeText7282148867920864569" style="display:none">Opening three years after the events of <u>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7898.Betsy_and_Joe__Betsy_Tacy___8_" title="Betsy and Joe (Betsy-Tacy, #8) by Maud Hart Lovelace" rel="nofollow noopener">Betsy and Joe</a>
</u>, the previous installment of Maud Hart Lovelace's <i>Betsy~Tacy</i> series, <u>Betsy and the Great World</u> sees its lovable heroine - now twenty-one years old, and about to be a junior in college - head off for a trip to Europe. Crossing the Atlantic on the Columbic ocean-liner, living in a student pension in Munich, exploring Venice with a handsome young man, and facing the onset of World War I with her friends in London, Betsy has many exciting adventures, and makes a diverse range of friends, from a German baroness to an Italian architect. She even meets that elusive type: the American millionaire!<br /><br />I enjoyed this grownup <i>Betsy~Tacy</i> adventure immensely, although - as my friend Wendy warned me - I did find some of the changes a little jarring. Had the Rays moved away from Deep Valley, I wondered, when Betsy references their home in Minneapolis? Betsy and Joe seemed to have such a wonderful understanding, at the close of the previous book, and although Lovelace explained how they drifted apart, it was still a little disappointing to see their estrangement! I also couldn't help but wish that there were some intermediate titles, covering Betsy's college years, although if they <i>were</i> so unhappy, perhaps it's for the best that the author glossed over them.<br /><br />Despite these concerns, I soon settled into this delightful tale of travel, which has always been one of my own passions. Lovelace writes beautifully, capturing a pre-war Europe that was soon to disappear, and inserting many fascinating social observations into her narrative. The idea of discrete social classes, held by both Tilda and Helena, is very foreign to Betsy, and I was rather impressed that Lovelace didn't allow her intrepid American to "resolve" everything, and make everyone friends. I also relished Betsy' passionate defense of the cause of woman suffrage, and her rejection of Mr. O'Farrell's false dichotomy of the feminine and the feminist.<br /><br />All in all, <u>Betsy and the Great World</u> was a great book - entertaining, thought-provoking, and emotionally satisfying. Especially, as another friend notes, that wonderful closing chapter! I can't wait to read <u>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7890.Betsy_s_Wedding__Betsy_Tacy___10_" title="Betsy's Wedding (Betsy-Tacy, #10) by Maud Hart Lovelace" rel="nofollow noopener">Betsy's Wedding</a>
</u>!</span>
  <a data-text-id="7282148867920864569" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="J. Boo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31060683-j-boo"><img alt="J. Boo" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1425446699p2/31060683.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3297601485?book_show_action=true">May 13, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J. Boo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J. Boo" href="/user/show/31060683-j-boo">J. Boo</a>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer3297601485" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer2642078541190198256">Betsy's travels and her minor adventures are great fun, but her love life isn't. I suspect that's because whenever Lovelace relays something that actually happened to her, or happened to one of her close group of friends, she does a smashing job, but she tends to flounder when she needs to make something up out of whole cloth. (Though, not having a thorough grounding in Lovelaceiana, it is also possible that I'm attributing to reality only the things that happened which I liked, and chalking up </span>
  <span id="freeText2642078541190198256" style="display:none">Betsy's travels and her minor adventures are great fun, but her love life isn't. I suspect that's because whenever Lovelace relays something that actually happened to her, or happened to one of her close group of friends, she does a smashing job, but she tends to flounder when she needs to make something up out of whole cloth. (Though, not having a thorough grounding in Lovelaceiana, it is also possible that I'm attributing to reality only the things that happened which I liked, and chalking up to to Lovelace's imagination things that I didn't.)</span>

<div id="review_2767464245" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2767464245" />
    <a title="Rebekah Giese Witherspoon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25938648-rebekah-giese-witherspoon"><img alt="Rebekah Giese Witherspoon" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1551978985p2/25938648.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2767464245?book_show_action=true">Jul 27, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rebekah Giese Witherspoon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rebekah Giese Witherspoon" href="/user/show/25938648-rebekah-giese-witherspoon">Rebekah Giese Witherspoon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2767464245" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16946454373773018760">JANUARY 1914. In those last halcyon days just before the world went mad, Betsy boarded the S.S. Columbic with dreams of living in Europe for the following year.<br/><br/>AUGUST 1914. While Betsy was living in England, the Great War began.<br/><br/>This novel is autobiographical, like all the books in the Betsy-Tacy series. Just like Betsy, Maud Hart Lovelace started a magnificently gorgeous European tour in January 1914, was living in London when World War I started, and had to return home to the US early. She live</span>
  <span id="freeText16946454373773018760" style="display:none">JANUARY 1914. In those last halcyon days just before the world went mad, Betsy boarded the S.S. Columbic with dreams of living in Europe for the following year.<br /><br />AUGUST 1914. While Betsy was living in England, the Great War began.<br /><br />This novel is autobiographical, like all the books in the Betsy-Tacy series. Just like Betsy, Maud Hart Lovelace started a magnificently gorgeous European tour in January 1914, was living in London when World War I started, and had to return home to the US early. She lived it, she felt it, and she shared it with me in this book. I loved it, and I'm so grateful for the experience.<br /><br />Most of this novel is filled with wide-eyed wonder and light-hearted European adventures, evoking the feeling and atmosphere of some of the most wonderful places I’ve ever visited.<br /><br />Munich: <i>She went to the Hoftheatre straight from afternoon coffee, for the operas began early, sometimes as early as six. There was always a line of people waiting for “standing places”—shabby, humble-looking people, and soldiers, and students. Yet inside, the great auditorium glittered and shimmered with fashion. Everybody went to the opera in Munich.</i><br /><br />Venice: <i>There were no streets except canals. Walking, you went up and down bridges, and along tiny alleys, spanned by clotheslines full of washing, and across picturesque courts where people were always hanging out the windows in vigorous conversation. With Mr. Regali, Betsy explored every tantalizing nook. And always in the end they came out on the Grand Canal. The palaces lifted their airy arches, balconies, and columns above water that changed color all the time.</i><br /><br />Only in the last 50 pages of the book do we start hearing the rumblings of war. My heart was in my throat during this section of the book, feeling all the feels.<br /><br />Betsy worries about her dear friends in Germany: <i>She thought of Tilda. What would this do to her [opera] career? She thought of Helena and Hanni. Each loved a soldier who would now be going to war.</i> <br /><br />And she worries about her dear friends in England, who are on the opposite side of the conflict. One of her British friends expresses it perfectly: <i>“I can’t take it in,” Jean stammered. “Thousands of men marching off to be slaughtered. Ruin, terror, misery, sweeping us all. Why?” No one knew.</i><br /><br />And how have I never heard about this mad-dash escape from Europe? <i>Americans were rushing off the continent like leaves before a storm. They weren’t allowed to leave the railroad stations, even to eat. They were locked into the cars. They couldn’t get money. Many who arrived in London had left all their luggage behind and had only the clothes on their backs.</i><br /><br />Confession: I skipped the previous two books in this series (Betsy Was a Junior and Betsy and Joe) because they sounded like plots recycled from Heaven to Betsy, which I didn’t enjoy. I’m so glad I didn’t give up on the Betsy-Tacy series. This book is a treasure and it’s officially on my “Want to Reread” list.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16946454373773018760" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_93168862" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/93168862" />
    <a title="Anne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/42321-anne"><img alt="Anne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1388463861p2/42321.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/93168862?book_show_action=true">Mar 08, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anne" href="/user/show/42321-anne">Anne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer93168862&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer93168862" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15293011804044133464">I can't remember the last time I cried in public. Maybe in the movie theater, maybe at summer camp. But if someone had told me at the beginning of <i>Betsy and the Great World</i> that two hundred some odd pages later I'd be sobbing in Barnes and Noble, I would not have believed it.<br/><br/>I spent a lot of the book, charming though it was, thinking that it wasn't as good as the Deep Valley books. I missed the Crowd, Mr. Ray's onion sandwiches, Tacy and Tib. It was definitely fun to read about Betsy's travels t</span>
  <span id="freeText15293011804044133464" style="display:none">I can't remember the last time I cried in public. Maybe in the movie theater, maybe at summer camp. But if someone had told me at the beginning of <i>Betsy and the Great World</i> that two hundred some odd pages later I'd be sobbing in Barnes and Noble, I would not have believed it.<br /><br />I spent a lot of the book, charming though it was, thinking that it wasn't as good as the Deep Valley books. I missed the Crowd, Mr. Ray's onion sandwiches, Tacy and Tib. It was definitely fun to read about Betsy's travels through Europe, her attraction to the purser Mr. O'Farrell, her new friends in Munich, her trip to the town with the Passion Play, the handsome Italian Marco in Venice...et cetera. But it got bogged down in the middle with historical detail, with descriptions of the new places. With Deep Valley, you just know where everything is, what it all looks like. I was also distracted by thinking about Tacy and how annoyed I was that she got married so young, and the fact that the cover still says "A Betsy-Tacy Story." The books stopped being about Tacy ages ago. I'm thinking about writing an essay about Tacy.<br /> <br />But then, WAR IS DECLARED and I spent the whole last two chapters crying. The scene in which Betsy and her London crew are sitting all together on her bed listening to the noise outside and waiting for the Bank Holiday to begin, and then they hear cries of "War!" was one of the most heartwrenching depictions of that event I have ever read (and I love books about this period!). The thing is, I guess I knew it was coming. Maybe that's why I didn't feel engaged in the rest of the book, because when Betsy and Tilda vowed to meet up again in Germany in 1917 (<i>neunzehn siebzehn!</i>), I thought to myself, "Wait, but..." And I didn't like it.<br /><br />And then I started thinking, aren't all of the Deep Valley boys going to go fight in Europe? More tears. Chest heaving, with the strains of Natalie Merchant in the background, staring at a rack of Wheel of Time novels. And of course, in the last two pages, the Agony Column from Joe: <i>Betsy. The great war is on but I hope ours is over. Please come home. Joe.</i> Devastating. And so beautiful.<br /><br />I'm looking forward to going back to Deep Valley for <i>Betsy's Wedding</i>, but I can't get over the punch to the gut that was the end of <i>Betsy and the Great World</i>. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15293011804044133464" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText15378843672331873494" style="display:none">Still haven't been to Venice, but I re-read this while in Rome.<br /><br />5-14-2009 review:<br />Oh how I love this book. I haven't yet been to Venice but, when I visited Munich in 2004, I used Betsy's explorations as my itinerary. It was the perfect way to see the city.<br /><br /></span>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/79398852?book_show_action=true">Nov 30, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Heidi-Marie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Heidi-Marie" href="/user/show/921817-heidi-marie">Heidi-Marie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/921817-heidi-marie?shelf=2010-book-challenge">2010-book-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/921817-heidi-marie?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/921817-heidi-marie?shelf=2010-ya-book-challenge">2010-ya-book-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/921817-heidi-marie?shelf=series">series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/921817-heidi-marie?shelf=ya-12-19">ya-12-19</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/921817-heidi-marie?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/921817-heidi-marie?shelf=own-in-the-home">own-in-the-home</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/921817-heidi-marie?shelf=biography">biography</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer79398852" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2436969757727605727">Love-hate relationship is too strong a phrase for this book. How about love-annoyed? Then again, I seem to have had that with Betsy all along in the series. The annoyed part was related to the male aspect. Again, that seems to be a trend. Yet it was a very good growing up part for Betsy and I think these were some of the greatest lessons in that area she has gained in the series. (I suppose that is like life for us, though--we learn more in various areas as we get older and have more experiences</span>
  <span id="freeText2436969757727605727" style="display:none">Love-hate relationship is too strong a phrase for this book. How about love-annoyed? Then again, I seem to have had that with Betsy all along in the series. The annoyed part was related to the male aspect. Again, that seems to be a trend. Yet it was a very good growing up part for Betsy and I think these were some of the greatest lessons in that area she has gained in the series. (I suppose that is like life for us, though--we learn more in various areas as we get older and have more experiences.) It also slightly annoyed me that she had some of her vain moments, and I was relieved that many parts of her trip had her "doing without."<br /><br />That aside, I loved the book. I loved being with Betsy on her adventures, and being reminded of my own venture into the Great World. I regretted and still regret that I could not spend more time in an area and actually live there to truly know the place and the people, so I envied Betsy her chances there. However, I could see even more how grateful I was to be in a tour group with so many friends and acquaintances to share my experiences, as compared to the times Betsy was alone or the effort she had to put forth to find friends in foreign lands. She probably grew more than I did in that regard, but I see that both of our Great World experiences were very eye-opening and life-altering.<br /><br />I enjoyed "visiting" some of the places I knew with Betsy, and really enjoyed going to new places. It was another reminder to me of how much I loved traveling and still long to go traveling. A whole year for Europe would be ideal. Give me another for Russia and another for Asia, and I'd have only just begun!<br /><br />I think what stood out to me most in this book, however, was the role of war. Considering how lightly the rest of the series had dealt with world affairs and knowing WWI was pending, I wasn't sure what would happen. But the book foreshadowed it as only a Betsy-Tacy book could. And I loved that the people, the cultures, and the places were much more real to Betsy--making the conflicts in the countries that much more prevalent and "meaningful" to her. (Especially considering 2 of 3 countries that she lived in, and the parts they would soon play in WWI.)<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[And the ending--I knew the Agony Column was going to play a part. But it still gave me the biggest grin as I read it. Wonderful ending (and beginning!) to a wonderful journey for Betsy. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />The rating is more of a 4.25 stars.<br /><br />P.S. Did I ever miss Tacy--but I SO knew what was going to happen when Betsy was in the doll shop! <br /><br />P.P.S. I never was impressed by Marco. In the least. Yet I liked Mr. Brown right off! Is that the author's doing or a sense of my "taste?"<br /><br />2/6/16-2/17/16 Reread! I think I enjoyed this much more this time around. Knowing how Betsy is, she didn't annoy me in the least. I actually love how honest Maud is about her own self through Betsy. Again found myself reveling in the same places Betsy and I both visited, wishing I could spend weeks at a time instead of 1-2 days, and longing to travel more. I also had refreshed myself with the history at the back of the book as to what was real and what was fiction. And since my last read, I have been a fan of Downton. So more parts stood out to me than they did before--the whole thing about upper class, maids, etc. It is sometimes uncanny to think that the times of Betsy and Meet Me in St. Louis were during the same time as Downton pre-war. Such different worlds! And to live in a country where class systems had that elements. (Though the U.S. didn't have royalty, it still had class divisions to a degree.) Anyway, my study of the Edwardian period over the last few years definitely came in to play as I re-read this book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2436969757727605727" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText9471036530205725218" style="display:none">This is probably my least favorite of the older Betsy-Tacy books. Just because Joe isn't really in it. Yes, the ending is really sweet ... but for most of the story <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Betsy and Joe aren't together! And BETSY IS PRACTICALLY GOING AFTER OTHER MEN. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I do love all the descriptions of different people and places. It really makes you want to visit Europe ... though not nowadays. I basically want to travel back to the pre-WW1 era just to visit Europe. Because I feel like it just wouldn't be any fun nowadays.<br /><br />I wish we could hear how all of the friends Betsy made in Europe ended up. For instance, Helena (if I remember the name correctly). She has such a tragic story, and we never get to hear how that ends up! So sad. (Yet another reason for more Betsy-Tacy books ...)<br /><br />Overall, this is a great story ... but not my favorite. I love the conclusions Betsy comes to. I always love seeing inside her head. :)<br /><br />~Kellyn Roth, <a href="https://reveriesreviews.wordpress.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">Reveries Reviews</a></span>

  <span id="freeText12891522067536500152" style="display:none">"After Commencement Day, the World!" Joe said. "With Betsy."<br />Oops. Joe forgot about Betsy being a flirt,(enter new guys)and about Betsy's social distractions.<br />Dropping out of college to travel made this a wonderful book..a look at Europe in the early 1900's before the War. Traveling alone around N.Africa, middle east and Europe was a brazen thing for a young lady to do even if she had escorts in place. The only thing that made Tib not being a part of this due to marriage and baby tolerable is that later in life Maude and her friend Bick (aka Tib) did travel to Europe together and see the world.<br />That Betsy and Joe FINALLY hook back up through the Agony Column in the London newspaper (basically an early personal ad ) was the icing on the cake! This is the book that I had remembered less of on my many previous readings, so was a treat to reread this time. <br />The only bad part of the book is now there is only one more left.......I can hardly bare the thought.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer18355754897852378683">We skip two years before this book begins, during which time Betsy went to University, hated it, dropped out, and lost touch with Joe when he went off to Harvard. And so, Bob Ray being the kind father that he is, offers a "snoggestion" that Betsy take some time to explore Europe.<br/><br/>So this book differs from the rest of the series, set alternately on an ocean liner across the Atlantic, in Italy, in Munich, in Paris, and in London. Betsy makes friends, falls in love with an Italian musician, socializ</span>
  <span id="freeText18355754897852378683" style="display:none">We skip two years before this book begins, during which time Betsy went to University, hated it, dropped out, and lost touch with Joe when he went off to Harvard. And so, Bob Ray being the kind father that he is, offers a "snoggestion" that Betsy take some time to explore Europe.<br /><br />So this book differs from the rest of the series, set alternately on an ocean liner across the Atlantic, in Italy, in Munich, in Paris, and in London. Betsy makes friends, falls in love with an Italian musician, socializes with socialites and cleaning staff alike, all while the shadow of the Great War creeps across Europe. And then the war begins, and Betsy makes plans to come home. And in the newspaper, a personal ad from Joe Willard reads: The Great War is on but ours is over. Come home. Joe."<br /><br />And so she does. </span>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review712858415">
  <td colspan="2">

<div id="review_2016536310" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2016536310" />
    <a title="Beth Bonini" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3106785-beth-bonini"><img alt="Beth Bonini" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1444482564p2/3106785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2016536310?book_show_action=true">Jun 01, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Beth Bonini" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Beth Bonini" href="/user/show/3106785-beth-bonini">Beth Bonini</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3106785-beth-bonini?shelf=friendship">friendship</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3106785-beth-bonini?shelf=london">london</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3106785-beth-bonini?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3106785-beth-bonini?shelf=world-war-i">world-war-i</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3106785-beth-bonini?shelf=travel">travel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3106785-beth-bonini?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3106785-beth-bonini?shelf=journey">journey</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2016536310" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15295364295074795740">When I was a teenager, I was in love with the idea of the 'year abroad' - not so much backpacking and hostels, but more along the lines of the glorious European Grand Tour. Who knows what books formed my romantic notions, but I'm sure this would have been one of them. This novel, the penultimate in the Betsy-Tacy series, is an anomaly in some ways: no Tacy and Tib, very little of the Ray family, and almost nothing of Joe Willard. The family closeness, the Ray family rituals, the seasonal traditi</span>
  <span id="freeText15295364295074795740" style="display:none">When I was a teenager, I was in love with the idea of the 'year abroad' - not so much backpacking and hostels, but more along the lines of the glorious European Grand Tour. Who knows what books formed my romantic notions, but I'm sure this would have been one of them. This novel, the penultimate in the Betsy-Tacy series, is an anomaly in some ways: no Tacy and Tib, very little of the Ray family, and almost nothing of Joe Willard. The family closeness, the Ray family rituals, the seasonal traditions - all of them are missing from this book. But what the reader gets instead is a fascinating slice of social history, and a glimpse of what Europe was like in the prosperous decades before World War I. It begins with a Betsy who has lost her 'grounding' in many ways. She has completed two fairly unsatisfactory years at the U (University of Minnesota in Minneapolis), she has fallen out with Joe, and she has more or less lost her way. It's a painful time for Betsy, and I think it's a painful time for her loyal readers, too! Both of us (Betsy, and the reader) have to adjust; we have to have our horizons gradually broadened. The novel follows Betsy's progress through Europe: first her long ship journey, with a stopover at the Azores and a crush on a charming Irish purser; then her time in Munich, where she befriends two German girls from very different social classes; then her trips to Bayreuth and Oberammergau; another romance in Venice; and finally, brief visits to Paris and London. While Betsy is in London, the war breaks out and she scrambles to get passage back home. I'm not quite sure how to classify this book; in some sense, the older books could be described as classic Young Adult, but I think this book will appeal mostly to adult readers who have some context for the people and places described in the book. It's not the most emotionally satisfying of the Betsy-Tacy books, but it is a unique pleasure to read. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15295364295074795740" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2195469141" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2195469141" />
    <a title="Jen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24377537-jen"><img alt="Jen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1380846068p2/24377537.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2195469141?book_show_action=true">Dec 03, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jen" href="/user/show/24377537-jen">Jen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780613035149" href="/book/show/1844657.Betsy_and_the_Great_World">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2195469141" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5222183283717077272">Ok, breaking it down:<br/><br/>I wouldn't have picked it up out of a store, probably - or out of the library. But I'm really glad I read it. I think I would have loved it when I was 10 or 11. The friendships would have kept me reading the whole series, I expect. <br/><br/>It really played the long game with Joe (and the resolution I know happened because of the tip-off of the next book's title in the back of this one is a good conclusion). <br/><br/>I think it's always interesting to write with a foreknowledge of circumstanc</span>
  <span id="freeText5222183283717077272" style="display:none">Ok, breaking it down:<br /><br />I wouldn't have picked it up out of a store, probably - or out of the library. But I'm really glad I read it. I think I would have loved it when I was 10 or 11. The friendships would have kept me reading the whole series, I expect. <br /><br />It really played the long game with Joe (and the resolution I know happened because of the tip-off of the next book's title in the back of this one is a good conclusion). <br /><br />I think it's always interesting to write with a foreknowledge of circumstances in historical fiction for younger readers so the hinting at German soldiers was deftly done. Also, my birthday is on June 28 so the murder of Franz Ferdinand is something I think about sometimes and I liked that it was in here specifically when so few dates are. <br /><br />The character of Betsy seems exactly like what Louisa May Alcott would have come up with if she had been alive and writing when Maud Hart Lovelace was so it was also great that Little Women was mentioned (2x) in the book. <br /><br />I really liked that the nuances between types of love and degrees of love were explored. The overall values of the Betsy (and the author) I could get behind - she was a very thoughtful and socially conscious writer. <br /><br />My mom growing up loved the book Marjorie Morningstar which was written around the same time period (also about a strong willed young woman who wants to be an artist - actress instead of writer). It was written by Herman Wouk (so, a man). In that book, in what has always seemed to me to be a not uncommon approach to 1950's values (especially from a man's perspective; and that something like The Feminine Mystique would have been an answer to), Marjorie gets any angst or desire for more satiated when she falls in and out of love with Noel Airman before settling into the<br />inevitability of domesticity. And absolutely, there is nothing wrong with any choice a person makes about their life circumstances. <br /><br />But, when I read that book as a pre-teen I was disappointed. She goes to live in a way she had never seemed to truly want, until she did. And she could have had more, she just didn't take her own aspirations seriously because no one else had, or if they did - it was only to use her. <br /><br />In this book, I love that Betsy maintains her individuality and passion and insistence on her own dreams as career worthy. AND I LOVE THAT PEOPLE IN HER LIFE SUPPORT HER. <br /><br />She has an admirable amount of agency and foresight and is a great character/would have been a great role model when I was a kid/might be a great role model now, too. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5222183283717077272" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2405421542">
  <td colspan="2">

<div id="review_195847387" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/195847387" />
    <a title="Lawrence" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1850370-lawrence"><img alt="Lawrence" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/195847387?book_show_action=true">Aug 07, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lawrence" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lawrence" href="/user/show/1850370-lawrence">Lawrence</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer195847387" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15377905709625819370">"Betsy and the Wide World" is not one of Ms. Lovelace's finest. The reason: After Betsy's absence from a couple of the intervening Deep Valley books, she returns to the scene essentially unchanged from what she was in high school. And even after her European tour described in this Wide World book, she remains unchanged. I am sorry to conclude that, to me, Betsy is an unlikeable and boring girl. She was more interesting when her age was in the single digits.<br/><br/>In Betsy and the Great World, we dive w</span>
  <span id="freeText15377905709625819370" style="display:none">"Betsy and the Wide World" is not one of Ms. Lovelace's finest. The reason: After Betsy's absence from a couple of the intervening Deep Valley books, she returns to the scene essentially unchanged from what she was in high school. And even after her European tour described in this Wide World book, she remains unchanged. I am sorry to conclude that, to me, Betsy is an unlikeable and boring girl. She was more interesting when her age was in the single digits.<br /><br />In Betsy and the Great World, we dive with Betsy from one shallow puddle into another. Her observations about the interesting places she visits --- The Azores, Morocco, Munich, etc. --- are superficial. She does not ever explore below the surface of what she sees. Witness her wide eyed innocence in merely asking "Why do they hate us so?" when she visits Tangiers briefly. Fortunately, she need not dwell on the question (even if she were so inclined) because she can return to the ship and revel in thinking about what she'll wear to some ball or other.<br /><br />In sum, Betsy is a passive observer. She looks at the world with an unchanging frame of reference, and the great world does not appear to affect her. I do not mean to diminish her ability to negotiate travel in foreign countries as a "girl alone". (Her aloneness is nominal, however, as there is always someone to take her under his/her wing, or to fall in love with her, etc.) But she remains essentially the same high school girl that she was in many other books --- the one who seeks "fun", who wants to belong to a set, to impress with airs and affectations, etc. She is really quite a dull child. And although she may be popular with her peers, adults seem really simply to indulge her.<br /><br />It is remarkable to me that Ms. Lovelace portrays Betsy as a young woman aspiring to a career as an author of fiction. Betsy's writing must indeed be of the most conventional sort, suitable for publication in popular magazines. (I did a google search of Ainslee's magazine, which paid Betsy $100 for a story.) Her desire to soak up atmosphere and visit places because it would help her writing is simply a pose. It is in this light that Ms. Lovelace has her visit the fashion show at Longschamps in Paris. Please! Betsy has no interior life out of which she could write. If she did, Mr. Ray could have saved his hard earned money and kept her home in Minneapolis. On the other hand, without an interior life, perhaps a trip to Europe was in Betsy's professional interest. At least she could collect charming locations.<br /><br />It is remarkable to me that Joe Willard --- who is a person of character --- could actually be charmed by this lover of hats, mirrors, and silk kimonos. I suspect that it is Joe who will provide Betsy with "character" in the next and last of the Betsy-Tacy series.<br /><br />Actually, I've really enjoyed reading this series. I suppose I would have stopped if I didn't. Also, I wouldn't feel so strongly about Betsy if Ms. Lovelace hadn't drawn me in so well!</span>
  <a data-text-id="15377905709625819370" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText17219843744682206279" style="display:none">This was the one I wasn't interested in when I was a young teen because I thought what could be more boring than to read about someone's trip around the world?     <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3331039558" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3331039558" />
    <a title="Logan Hughes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15052079-logan-hughes"><img alt="Logan Hughes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1566699500p2/15052079.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3331039558?book_show_action=true">May 11, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Logan Hughes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Logan Hughes" href="/user/show/15052079-logan-hughes">Logan Hughes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3331039558" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6979148902762355297">I'm a sucker for the later Betsy books, and this one is one of my favorites. It's two years after Betsy's high school graduation and in the interim, she has given college the old college try, left due to illness, and chosen not to go back. Instead, she is using the rest of her college money to travel the world and get experiences to write about. We follow Betsy on an trip through Europe. There's a new crew and new dramas in each place. Of course, being who she is, she flirts with a purser on the</span>
  <span id="freeText6979148902762355297" style="display:none">I'm a sucker for the later Betsy books, and this one is one of my favorites. It's two years after Betsy's high school graduation and in the interim, she has given college the old college try, left due to illness, and chosen not to go back. Instead, she is using the rest of her college money to travel the world and get experiences to write about. We follow Betsy on an trip through Europe. There's a new crew and new dramas in each place. Of course, being who she is, she flirts with a purser on the cruise ship on her way out. Once settled in her first stop, in Munich, she learns to overcome homesickness and small fears, and rely on her talent for friend-making. In Italy, she falls in love again, in a dreamy romantic sequence. Particularly memorable is her trip through the Bavarian countryside where she visits a number of idyllic and unusual towns, such as one built around doll-making, and one that puts on a passion play each year, with people known by the roles they play. The story ends dramatically - but predictably, if you're paying attention to the dates - when World War I breaks out, forcing Betsy to make a hasty end to her trip and return home before it becomes impossible. Suddenly, what was a cozy story of small-scale dramas becomes, in retrospect, a bittersweet farewell to entire way of life. The world that Betsy discovered on her journey has already changed forever. It gives me chills is what it does. <br /><br />I really enjoy seeing Betsy as a young adult. She's believably early 20s and recognizably herself - plucky, determined, flirty, fun, friendly, sometimes moody, with a weakness for dramatic self-pity. But even her scenes of homesickness feel comforting; she is not perfect and feels just I would, and just as I have, embarking on a big scary new experience. But she also knows, from experiences and skills you've seen her build along the way, how to pull herself out of a funk, make friends in any situation, and get down to work with discipline. I first read these books as a young adult, about the same age Betsy is here, and this is the book where she felt most like a peer. Now that I'm even older, there's almost a sense of parental pride in reading this and seeing how nicely she turned out. <br /><br />This book is extremely pleasant, and its sweet nostalgic quality is enhanced by the dark notes both at the beginning and the end. In the years skipped over by the narrative, Betsy has essentially failed out of college. We've followed this girl for her entire childhood as she dreams of success and happiness, and she's experienced a serious setback. This trip is part comeback, part convalescence, part desperate Hail Mary. <br /><br />At the end of the book, things begin to change very rapidly with the start of the war (having lived through a few world-changing events myself, the shift from "huh something is happening in the news" to "it isn't really serious though is it" to "oh shit I need to change everything" over a few days feels very realistically told). The entire book has been about Betsy broadening her horizons and widening the scope of her life - getting out of the small town where she's always lived and seeing the world and how other people live - and the war very suddenly broadens the scope further. Betsy can no longer focus on her own day-to-day dramas; failing out of college seems like small potatoes now. She has become aware of the direct effect of world events on her own life and also begins to ponder how she might affect them in turn. <br /><br />Lovelace based this book on her own year in Europe in 1914, so it's not as though she chose this moment in history on purpose for literary reasons. Still, it's a wonderful metaphor for the transition between child and teenagerhood and the responsibilities of adulthood. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6979148902762355297" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3034428192" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3034428192" />
    <a title="Kiana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36504237-kiana"><img alt="Kiana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567452711p2/36504237.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3034428192?book_show_action=true">Nov 02, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kiana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kiana" href="/user/show/36504237-kiana">Kiana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36504237-kiana?shelf=potential-oomph-but-no-oomph">potential-oomph-but-no-oomph</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36504237-kiana?shelf=decent-or-even-good-sequels">decent-or-even-good-sequels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36504237-kiana?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36504237-kiana?shelf=historical">historical</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3034428192" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14447385266798706086">Although <i>Betsy and the Great World</i> sometimes feels a bit too much like reading someone’s travel diary for my liking, it’s also the first book that is truly different from its predecessors: for the first time, Betsy leaves the comfort of her Deep Valley home and isn’t surrounded by a crowd of adoring and supportive friends and family, and the result is her having to struggle to find her own way in a foreign land. Of course, given the series’ comfortable tone, Betsy’s troubles never last long but </span>
  <span id="freeText14447385266798706086" style="display:none">Although <i>Betsy and the Great World</i> sometimes feels a bit too much like reading someone’s travel diary for my liking, it’s also the first book that is truly different from its predecessors: for the first time, Betsy leaves the comfort of her Deep Valley home and isn’t surrounded by a crowd of adoring and supportive friends and family, and the result is her having to struggle to find her own way in a foreign land. Of course, given the series’ comfortable tone, Betsy’s troubles never last long but it was the first time I felt like the character really moved forward since <i>Heaven to Betsy</i> (if even that). <br /><br />Easily the most effective part of the story for me was the beginning of World War I, but I also feel as though the book missed an opportunity here. Despite the enormity of the event, and Betsy being in London right as people began to panic, it never seemed as though Betsy processed the significance of what was happening, instead viewing it as she did all of the other tourist sights that she came across. Perhaps this, like the loving descriptions of all of the countries Betsy visits (many of which will be affected by World War I), is meant to be a bittersweet love letter to the time before the Great War and a sad look at how unaware everyone was of what was about to go down. But it also left things feeling rather odd at the end—a huge, history-changing development occurs, yet Betsy just sails home to America to have a romantic reunion with her high school sweetheart like the world is the same. Maybe the last book (which is set right after this one) will have more gravity, but I somewhat doubt it; then again, these books are clearly intended as comforting escapism so ruminating about them being anything much deeper than that might be somewhat pointless. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14447385266798706086" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2400945539" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2400945539" />
    <a title="Crizzle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8809043-crizzle"><img alt="Crizzle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1486221152p2/8809043.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2400945539?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Crizzle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Crizzle" href="/user/show/8809043-crizzle">Crizzle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8809043-crizzle?shelf=chic-lit">chic-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8809043-crizzle?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8809043-crizzle?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2400945539" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16800108727086008567">It’s hard to read these teen/college Betsy books; I actually enjoy the forewords (this one was “Betsy Ray: Feminist Icon”) and the afterward better. In this afterward we learn that besides names, this book was basically all true to Maud Hart Lovelace’s life, as she used her letters home to write every part of Betsy’s European adventure. So even though I’m endlessly annoyed by Betsy (not to mention her kid-self is so much more interesting and funny), I do love her forever deep down. That is only </span>
  <span id="freeText16800108727086008567" style="display:none">It’s hard to read these teen/college Betsy books; I actually enjoy the forewords (this one was “Betsy Ray: Feminist Icon”) and the afterward better. In this afterward we learn that besides names, this book was basically all true to Maud Hart Lovelace’s life, as she used her letters home to write every part of Betsy’s European adventure. So even though I’m endlessly annoyed by Betsy (not to mention her kid-self is so much more interesting and funny), I do love her forever deep down. That is only due to the magical first four books. I only have one more in the series to go, “Betsy’s Wedding”, and thank goodness I know who her husband will be. I wanted to barf reading her flirtations with the older man on the ship, then obviously she found out he was married... then I was so irritated at her fling with Marco in Italy. But such is life. Luckily there was that ending - Joe! <br />Something I find hard to forgive is the flippant little episode with the doll. Betsy goes to a place famous for its dolls and she comes across child labor, which she feels uncomfortable with but hopes these children have their own dolls to play with. When she sees their tattered, banged-up, headless toys, she is relieved and feels no objection to treating herself to the fanciest doll she finds. It’s only when her pride makes her aware at how silly she must look walking along with a doll that I think, “Okay, now is when she’ll redeem herself and take it to the little girl with the headless doll.” She does nothing of the sort and the chapter ends, and I’m left baffled.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16800108727086008567" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/5306.Maud_Hart_Lovelace">About Maud Hart Lovelace</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/5306.Maud_Hart_Lovelace"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1362527109p3/5306.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/5306.Maud_Hart_Lovelace">Maud Hart Lovelace
</a></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer7910977555154039472">Maud Hart Lovelace was born on April 25, 1892, in Mankato, Minnesota. She was the middle of three children born to Thomas and Stella (Palmer) Hart. Her sister, Kathleen, was three years older, and her other sister, Helen, was six years younger. “That dear family" was the model for the fictional Ray family.<br/><br/>Maud’s birthplace was a small house on a hilly residential street several blocks above Mankat</span>
  <span id="freeText7910977555154039472" style="display:none">Maud Hart Lovelace was born on April 25, 1892, in Mankato, Minnesota. She was the middle of three children born to Thomas and Stella (Palmer) Hart. Her sister, Kathleen, was three years older, and her other sister, Helen, was six years younger. “That dear family" was the model for the fictional Ray family.<br /><br />Maud’s birthplace was a small house on a hilly residential street several blocks above Mankato’s center business district. The street, Center Street, dead-ended at one of the town’s many hills. When Maud was a few months old, the Hart family moved two blocks up the street to 333 Center. <br /><br />Shortly before Maud’s fifth birthday a “large merry Irish family" moved into the house directly across the street. Among its many children was a girl Maud’s age, Frances, nicknamed Bick, who was to be Maud’s best friend and the model for Tacy Kelly. <br /><br />Tib’s character was based on another playmate, Marjorie (Midge) Gerlach, who lived nearby in a large house designed by her architect father. Maud, Bick, and Midge became lifelong friends. Maud once stated that the three couldn’t have been closer if they’d been sisters.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7910977555154039472" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
